<p>The <b>French Grand Prix</b> (<a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a>: <i lang="fr">Grand Prix de France</i>), formerly known as the <b>Grand Prix de l'ACF</b>, is an <a href="/wiki/Auto_racing" title="Auto racing">auto race</a> held as part of the <a href="/wiki/F%C3%A9d%C3%A9ration_Internationale_de_l%27Automobile" title="Fédération Internationale de l&#39;Automobile">Fédération Internationale de l'Automobile</a>'s annual <a href="/wiki/Formula_One" title="Formula One">Formula One World Championship</a>. It is one of the oldest motor races in the world as well as the first "<a href="/wiki/Grand_Prix_motor_racing" title="Grand Prix motor racing">Grand Prix</a>". It ceased shortly after its centenary in <a href="/wiki/2008_Formula_One_World_Championship" title="2008 Formula One World Championship">2008</a> with 86 races having been held, due to unfavourable financial circumstances and venues. The race returned to the Formula One calendar in <a href="/wiki/2018_Formula_One_World_Championship" title="2018 Formula One World Championship">2018</a> with <a href="/wiki/Circuit_Paul_Ricard" title="Circuit Paul Ricard">Circuit Paul Ricard</a> hosting the race.
</p><p>Unusually even for a race of such longevity, the location of the Grand Prix has moved frequently with 16 different venues having been used over its life, a number only eclipsed by the 23 venues used for the <a href="/wiki/Australian_Grand_Prix" title="Australian Grand Prix">Australian Grand Prix</a> since its 1928 start. It is also one of four races (along with the <a href="/wiki/Belgian_Grand_Prix" title="Belgian Grand Prix">Belgian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Italian_Grand_Prix" title="Italian Grand Prix">Italian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Grand_Prix" title="Spanish Grand Prix">Spanish</a> Grands Prix) to have been held as part of the three distinct Grand Prix championships (<a href="/wiki/World_Manufacturers%27_Championship" title="World Manufacturers&#39; Championship">World Manufacturers' Championship</a> in the late 1920s, <a href="/wiki/European_Championship_(auto_racing)" class="mw-redirect" title="European Championship (auto racing)">European Championship</a> in the 1930s and Formula One World Championship since 1950).
</p><p>The Grand Prix de l'ACF was tremendously influential in the early years of Grand Prix racing, leading the establishment of the rules and regulations of racing as well as setting trends in the evolution of racing. The power of the original organiser, the <a href="/wiki/Automobile_Club_de_France" class="mw-redirect" title="Automobile Club de France">Automobile Club de France</a>, established France as the home of motor racing organisation.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=French_Grand_Prix&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Origins">Origins</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=French_Grand_Prix&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Origins">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>France was one of the first countries to hold motor racing events of any kind. The first competitive motor race, the <a href="/wiki/Paris%E2%80%93Rouen_(motor_race)" title="Paris–Rouen (motor race)">Paris to Rouen</a> Horseless Carriages Contest was held on 22 July 1894, and was organized by the Automobile Club de France (ACF). The race was 126&#160;km (78&#160;mi) long and was won by Count Jules-Albert de Dion in his <a href="/wiki/De_Dion_Bouton" class="mw-redirect" title="De Dion Bouton">De Dion Bouton</a> steam powered car in just under 7 hours. This race was followed by races starting in <a href="/wiki/Paris" title="Paris">Paris</a> to various towns and cities around France such as <a href="/wiki/Bordeaux" title="Bordeaux">Bordeaux</a>, <a href="/wiki/Marseille" title="Marseille">Marseille</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lyon" title="Lyon">Lyon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dieppe" title="Dieppe">Dieppe</a>, and also to various other European cities such as Amsterdam, Berlin, Innsbruck and Vienna. The 1901 Paris-Berlin race was noteworthy as the race winner, Henri Fouriner averaged an astonishing 57&#160;mph (93&#160;km/h) in his <a href="/wiki/Mors_(automobile)" title="Mors (automobile)">Mors</a>, but there were details of other incidents. A competitor driving a 40H.P. Panhard suddenly found the road blocked by a tram in the village of Metternich, and he deliberately ran into the vehicle to avoid the crowd of spectators. The tram was knocked off the rails; the car was hardly damaged. And in Reims, a future location of many French Grands Prix, another competitor hit and killed a child who wandered onto the road with his Mors.
</p><p>But these races, held on public dirt roads that were not all closed to the public came to a halt in 1903. The <a href="/wiki/Paris-Madrid_race" class="mw-redirect" title="Paris-Madrid race">Paris-Madrid race</a>, a 1,307&#160;km (812&#160;mi) long competition from the French capital to the Spanish capital held in May of that year had over 300 entrants. Some of the cars were doing 140&#160;km/h (87&#160;mph)- an astonishingly fast speed for the time- not even rail locomotives were capable of hitting these speeds. It was not known at the time how safe these races would be or how these cars- made mostly of wood would perform, and development of the car had improved significantly over 9 years. The race was a disaster, with 8 people killed and over 15 injured in multiple accidents- and all this happened before any of the competitors reached the Spanish border. Crowds of onlookers would stand right on the edge of the track, and children were wandering into the roads which became very dusty and visibility was limited at best. The most notable fatality of this race was one <a href="/wiki/Marcel_Renault" title="Marcel Renault">Marcel Renault</a>, one of the 3 brothers who founded <a href="/wiki/Renault" title="Renault">Renault Cars</a>. When Renault reached the village of <a href="/wiki/Payr%C3%A9" title="Payré">Payré</a> just south of <a href="/wiki/Poitiers" title="Poitiers">Grand Poitiers</a> he lost control of his 16HP Renault in poor visibility caused by excess dust. The car went into a gutter and crashed into a tree, and Renault sustained a horrific wound in the side of his head and dislocated his shoulder. Fellow competitor Leon Théry stopped his <a href="/wiki/Decauville" title="Decauville">Decauville</a> in order to help Renault and his riding-mechanic Vauthier, still trapped in their car. No doctors were on hand, but Théry found one at the next village and sent him to the place of accident- riding a bicycle to get there. The doctor conveyed back Renault to the nearest hospital in Grand Poitiers, where Renault succumbed to his injuries two days later, while Vauthier survived with minor injuries. Accidents continued throughout the day; cars hit trees and disintegrated, they overturned and caught fire, axles broke and inexperienced drivers crashed on the rough roads. The race was eventually called off by the French government and there was no declared winner. The cars were impounded by the French authorities, towed to the nearest rail stations by horses and transported back to Paris by train. The race created a political uproar in France, and a French magazine did their own investigation into the race. Speed, dust created by the cars, poor organization and lack of crowd control were to blame for these tragedies and even French Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/%C3%89mile_Combes" title="Émile Combes">Émile Combes</a> was held partially responsible because he was the ultimate authority on allowing the race to proceed.
</p><p>Other races were organized by American newspaper publisher James Gordon Bennett called the Gordon Bennett Cup, 4 of which were in France. 3 city-to-city races in 1900, 1901 and 1902, all starting in Paris were organized by Bennett and they attracted top racers from the United States and Western Europe. But after the 1903 Paris-Madrid race, the French government banned point-to-point car races, so Bennett moved the 1903 race to Ireland on a closed circuit 2 months after Paris-Madrid, the first of its kind. This race was won by Belgian <a href="/wiki/Camille_Jenatzy" title="Camille Jenatzy">Camille Jenatzy</a> in a Mercedes. The 1904 race was held in western Germany while the last Gordon Bennett Cup race was held in an 137&#160;km (85&#160;mi) circuit in <a href="/wiki/Auvergne" title="Auvergne">Auvergne</a> in south-central France. The race started in Clermont-Ferrand, and was run over 4 laps, and was won by Leon Théry in a <a href="/wiki/Brasier" title="Brasier">Brasier</a>.
</p>
<h3><span id="The_world.27s_oldest_Grand_Prix"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="The_world's_oldest_Grand_Prix">The world's oldest Grand Prix</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=French_Grand_Prix&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: The world&#039;s oldest Grand Prix">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Public_road_courses">Public road courses</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=French_Grand_Prix&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Public road courses">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Boillot-ACF-GP1912.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Boillot-ACF-GP1912.jpg/220px-Boillot-ACF-GP1912.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="156" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Boillot-ACF-GP1912.jpg/330px-Boillot-ACF-GP1912.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/c2/Boillot-ACF-GP1912.jpg 2x" data-file-width="374" data-file-height="265" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Boillot-ACF-GP1912.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Georges_Boillot" title="Georges Boillot">Georges Boillot</a> winning the 1912 French Grand Prix in Dieppe, France</div></div></div>
<p>The French <i>Grand Prix</i>, open to international competition was first run on 26 June 1906 under the auspices of the <a href="/wiki/Automobile_Club_de_France" class="mw-redirect" title="Automobile Club de France">Automobile Club de France</a> in <a href="/wiki/Sarthe" title="Sarthe">Sarthe</a> with a starting field of 32 automobiles. The Grand Prix name ("Great Prize") referred to the prize of 45,000 <a href="/wiki/French_franc" title="French franc">French francs</a> to the race winner.<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> The franc was pegged to the gold at 0.290 grams per franc, which meant that the prize was worth 13&#8202;kg of gold, or €191,000 adjusted for inflation. The earliest French Grands Prix were held on circuits consisting of public roads near towns through northern and central France, and they usually were held at different towns each year, such as Le Mans, Dieppe, Amiens, Lyon, Strasbourg, and Tours. Dieppe in particular was an extremely dangerous circuit – 9 people (5 drivers, 2 riding mechanics, and 2 spectators) in total were killed at the three French Grands Prix held at the 79&#8202;km (49-mile) circuit.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/1906_French_Grand_Prix" title="1906 French Grand Prix">The 1906 race</a> was the first ever race named "Grand Prix"; other, later, international events in the 1900s and 1910s in Europe and the United States had their own names with the term "Prize" in them, such as <i><a href="/wiki/United_States_Grand_Prix" title="United States Grand Prix">Grand Prize</a></i> in America or <i><a href="/wiki/Kaiserpreis" title="Kaiserpreis">Kaiserpreis</a></i> (English: Emperor's Prize) in Germany. The French Grand Prix race was run on a 66-mile (106&#8202;km) closed public road circuit starting at the western French town of <a href="/wiki/Le_Mans" title="Le Mans">Le Mans</a>, through a series of villages and back again to Le Mans. Hungarian <a href="/wiki/Ferenc_Szisz" title="Ferenc Szisz">Ferenc Szisz</a> won this very long 12&#x2011;hour race on a Renault from Italian <a href="/wiki/Felice_Nazzaro" title="Felice Nazzaro">Felice Nazzaro</a> in a <a href="/wiki/Fiat" title="Fiat">Fiat</a>, where laps on this circuit took around an hour and the horse carriage road surface was made of dirt. The 1908 race saw Mercedes humiliating the French organizers and finishing 1-2-3 at the lethal circuit at Dieppe, where no less than 4 people were killed during the weekend. The 1913 race was won by <a href="/wiki/Georges_Boillot" title="Georges Boillot">Georges Boillot</a> on a one-off 19-mile (31&#8202;km) circuit near Amiens in northern France. Amiens was another extremely deadly circuit – it had a 7.1 mile straight and 5 people were killed during its use during pre-race testing and the race weekend itself. The 1914 race, run on a 23&#x2011;mile circuit near Lyon is perhaps the most legendary Grand Prix of the pre&#x2011;WWI racing era. This circuit had a twisty and demanding section down to the town of Le Madeline and then an 8.3 mile straight which returned to the pits. This race was a hard-fought battle between the French Peugeots and the German Mercedes. Although the Peugeots were fast and Boillot ended up leading for 12 of the 20 laps the Dunlop tyres they used wore out badly compared to the Continentials that the Mercedes cars were using. Boillot's four-minute lead was wiped out by Christian Lautenschlager in a Mercedes while Boillot stopped an incredible eight times for tyres. Although Boillot drove very hard to try to catch Lautenschlager, he had to retire on the last lap due to engine failure, and for the second time in 6 years Mercedes finished 1–2–3; a humiliating result for the organizers and Peugeot.
</p><p>Thanks to World War I and the amount of damage it did to France, the Grand Prix was not brought back until 1921, and that race was won by American <a href="/wiki/Jimmy_Murphy_(racing_driver)" title="Jimmy Murphy (racing driver)">Jimmy Murphy</a> with a <a href="/wiki/Duesenberg" title="Duesenberg">Duesenberg</a> at the Sarthe circuit at Le Mans, which was the now legendary circuit's first year of operation. <a href="/wiki/Bugatti" title="Bugatti">Bugatti</a> made its debut at the 1922 race at an 8.3&#x2011;mile (13&#8202;km) off-public road circuit near Strasbourg near the French-German border – which was very close to Bugatti's headquarters in Molsheim. It rained, and the muddy circuit was in a dreadful condition. This race became a duel between Bugatti and Fiat – and Felice Nazzaro won in a Fiat, although his nephew and fellow competitor Biagio Nazzaro was killed after the axle on his Fiat broke, threw a wheel and hit a tree; the 32-year old and his riding mechanic both suffered fatal head injuries. The 1923 race at another one-off circuit near Tours featured another new Bugatti – the Type 32. This car was insultingly dubbed the "Tank", owing to its streamlined shape and very short wheelbase. This car was fast on the straights of this high-speed public road circuit – but it handled badly and was outpaced by Briton <a href="/wiki/Henry_Seagrave" class="mw-redirect" title="Henry Seagrave">Henry Seagrave</a> in a supercharged <a href="/wiki/Sunbeam_Motor_Car_Company#Production_cars" title="Sunbeam Motor Car Company">Sunbeam</a>, supercharging being common feature of Grand Prix cars during this period. Seagrave won the race, and the Sunbeam would be the last British car to win an official Grand Prix until <a href="/wiki/Stirling_Moss" title="Stirling Moss">Stirling Moss</a>'s victory with a <a href="/wiki/Vanwall" title="Vanwall">Vanwall</a> at the 1957 British Grand Prix. The 1924 race was held again at Lyon, but this time on a shortened 14&#x2011;mile variant of the circuit used in 1914. Two of the most successful Grand Prix cars of all time, the Bugatti Type 35 and the <a href="/wiki/Alfa_Romeo_P2" title="Alfa Romeo P2">Alfa Romeo P2</a> both made their debuts at this race. The Bugattis, with their advanced alloy wheels suffered tyre failure, and Italian Giuseppe Campari won in his Alfa P2.
</p>
<h4><span id="France.27s_first_permanent_circuit_and_other_public_road_circuits"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="France's_first_permanent_circuit_and_other_public_road_circuits">France's first permanent circuit and other public road circuits</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=French_Grand_Prix&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: France&#039;s first permanent circuit and other public road circuits">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a href="/wiki/File:French_GP_map.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/French_GP_map.png/400px-French_GP_map.png" decoding="async" width="400" height="375" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/French_GP_map.png/600px-French_GP_map.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/French_GP_map.png/800px-French_GP_map.png 2x" data-file-width="1000" data-file-height="938" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:French_GP_map.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Map of the French Grand Prix locations</div></div></div>
<p>In 1925, the first permanent autodrome in France was built, it was called <a href="/wiki/Autodrome_de_Linas-Montlh%C3%A9ry" title="Autodrome de Linas-Montlhéry">Autodrome de Linas-Montlhéry</a>, located 20 miles south of the centre of the French capital of Paris. The 7.7&#x2011;mile (12.3&#8202;km) circuit included a 51&#x2011;degree concrete banking, an asphalt road course and then-modern facilities, including pit garages and grandstands. After the construction of <a href="/wiki/Brooklands" title="Brooklands">Brooklands</a> in England in 1907, and Indianapolis in the United States in 1908 and after <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a>, Monza in Italy was opened in 1922, and <a href="/wiki/Aut%C3%B3dromo_de_Sitges-Terramar" title="Autódromo de Sitges-Terramar">Stiges–Terramar</a> in Spain was also opened in 1923. The French were then prompted to construct a purpose-built racing circuit at Montlhéry in the north and then Miramas in the south. <a href="/wiki/N%C3%BCrburgring" title="Nürburgring">Nürburgring</a> in Germany followed in 1927. Montlhery first held the Grand Prix de l'ACF in 1925 as part of the inaugural <a href="/wiki/World_Manufacturers%27_Championship" title="World Manufacturers&#39; Championship">World Manufacturers' Championship</a>, the first time Grands Prix were grouped together to form a championship. The circuit drew huge crowds and they were witnesses to the spectacular sight of fast cars racing on Montlhéry's steep banking and asphalt road course, which had many fast corners and long straights, and was located in a forest. The first race at Montlhéry was marred by the fatal accident of <a href="/wiki/Antonio_Ascari" title="Antonio Ascari">Antonio Ascari</a> in an Alfa P2. <a href="/wiki/Miramas#Circuit_of_Miramas" title="Miramas">Miramas</a>, a high-banked concrete oval track like Brooklands and part of Montlhéry was completed in 1926, and it played host to the Grand Prix that year. This race saw only three cars compete, all Bugattis, and was won by Frenchman <a href="/wiki/Jules_Goux" title="Jules Goux">Jules Goux</a> who had also won the <a href="/wiki/Indianapolis_500" title="Indianapolis 500">Indianapolis 500</a> in 1913.
</p><p>The 1927 race at Montlhéry was won by Frenchman <a href="/wiki/Robert_Benoist" title="Robert Benoist">Robert Benoist</a> in a <a href="/wiki/Delage" title="Delage">Delage</a>. 1929 saw a brief return to Le Mans, which was won by <a href="/wiki/William_Grover-Williams" title="William Grover-Williams">William Grover-Williams</a> in a Bugatti; this was the man who had won the first ever <a href="/wiki/Monaco_Grand_Prix" title="Monaco Grand Prix">Monaco Grand Prix</a> earlier in the year; Grover-Williams had also won the 1928 race in a Bugatti at the 17-mile (28&#160;km) Saint-Gaudens circuit in the south, not far from <a href="/wiki/Toulouse" title="Toulouse">Toulouse</a>. The 1930 French Grand Prix, held at Pau back down in the south was one of the more memorable French Grands Prix of the pre-World War II period. This race, held in September on a one-off triangular 9.8&#x2011;mile (15.8&#8202;-km) public road circuit just a few kilometres away from the current Pau Grand Prix track saw a special supercharged version of the famous <a href="/wiki/Bentley_4%C2%BD_Litre" title="Bentley 4½ Litre">Bentley 4½ Litre</a> called the <a href="/wiki/Bentley_4%C2%BD_Litre#Blower_Bentley" title="Bentley 4½ Litre">Blower Bentley</a> compete in the race with Briton and "Bentley Boy" <a href="/wiki/Tim_Birkin" title="Tim Birkin">Tim Birkin</a> driving. The Bentley team had been dominating the famed <a href="/wiki/24_Hours_of_Le_Mans" title="24 Hours of Le Mans">24 Hours of Le Mans</a>, and this Blower Bentley had its headlights and mudguards removed, as these were not needed for this race, giving it the appearance of an open-wheel car. The Bentley, which was much larger and heavier than the small Bugattis around it performed well – at this very fast circuit which was made up of very long straights and tight hairpins actually suited the powerful Blower Bentley, and it enabled Birkin to pass the pits at 130&#8202;mph (very fast for that time), and he overtook car after car – to the amazement of the crowd. But he finished second to Frenchman <a href="/wiki/Philippe_%C3%89tancelin" title="Philippe Étancelin">Philippe Étancelin</a> in a Bugatti.
</p><p>Montlhéry would also be part of the second Grand Prix championship era; the <a href="/wiki/European_Championship_(auto_racing)" class="mw-redirect" title="European Championship (auto racing)">European Championship</a> when it began in 1931. Other public road circuits near towns such as Reims also played host to French Grand Prix, such as the fast, straight and slow corner-dominated 4.8&#x2011;mile <a href="/wiki/Reims-Gueux" title="Reims-Gueux">Reims-Gueux</a> circuit in the <a href="/wiki/Champagne_wine_region" title="Champagne wine region">Champagne wine region</a> of Northern France 144&#8202;km (90&#8202;mi) east of Paris for 1932, where Italian legend <a href="/wiki/Tazio_Nuvolari" title="Tazio Nuvolari">Tazio Nuvolari</a> won in an Alfa Romeo. But from 1933–1937 Montlhéry would become the sole host of the event. The 1934 French Grand Prix marked the return of <a href="/wiki/Mercedes-Benz" title="Mercedes-Benz">Mercedes-Benz</a> to Grand Prix racing after 20 years, with an all-new car, team, management, and drivers, headed by <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Neubauer" title="Alfred Neubauer">Alfred Neubauer</a>. 1934 was the year where the German Silver Arrows debuted (an effort heavily funded by <a href="/wiki/Adolf_Hitler" title="Adolf Hitler">Hitler's</a> <a href="/wiki/Third_Reich" class="mw-redirect" title="Third Reich">Third Reich</a>), with Auto Union having already debuted its powerful mid-engined Type–A car for a race at <a href="/wiki/AVUS" title="AVUS">AVUS</a> in Germany. Although the Monégasque driver <a href="/wiki/Louis_Chiron" title="Louis Chiron">Louis Chiron</a> won in an Alfa, the Silver Arrows dominated the race. The high-tech German cars seemed to float over the rough concrete banking at Montlhéry where all the other cars seemed to be visibly affected by the concrete surface. Makeshift chicanes were placed at certain points on the very high-speed circuit in an effort by the French to slow the very fast German cars down for the 1935 race, but this effort came to nothing as Mercedes superstar <a href="/wiki/Rudolf_Caracciola" title="Rudolf Caracciola">Rudolf Caracciola</a> won that year's race.
</p>
<h4><span id="Reims.2C_Rouen_and_Charade"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Reims,_Rouen_and_Charade">Reims, Rouen and Charade</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=French_Grand_Prix&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Reims, Rouen and Charade">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Circuit_Charade_1958_1988.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Circuit_Charade_1958_1988.png/220px-Circuit_Charade_1958_1988.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="216" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Circuit_Charade_1958_1988.png/330px-Circuit_Charade_1958_1988.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Circuit_Charade_1958_1988.png/440px-Circuit_Charade_1958_1988.png 2x" data-file-width="1428" data-file-height="1402" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Circuit_Charade_1958_1988.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Charade circuit, used in 1965, 1969, 1970 and 1972</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Circuit_Reims-Gueux.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Circuit_Reims-Gueux.png/220px-Circuit_Reims-Gueux.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="229" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Circuit_Reims-Gueux.png/330px-Circuit_Reims-Gueux.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Circuit_Reims-Gueux.png/440px-Circuit_Reims-Gueux.png 2x" data-file-width="470" data-file-height="490" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Circuit_Reims-Gueux.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The faster Reims circuit, used from 1953 to 1966</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Rouen_track_layout_1955-1971.gif" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Rouen_track_layout_1955-1971.gif/220px-Rouen_track_layout_1955-1971.gif" decoding="async" width="220" height="130" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Rouen_track_layout_1955-1971.gif/330px-Rouen_track_layout_1955-1971.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Rouen_track_layout_1955-1971.gif/440px-Rouen_track_layout_1955-1971.gif 2x" data-file-width="860" data-file-height="508" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Rouen_track_layout_1955-1971.gif" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Rouen-Les-Essarts, used in 1952 (shortened), 1957, 1962, 1964 and 1968</div></div></div>
<p>The French Grand Prix returned to the Reims-Gueux circuit for 1938 and 1939, where the Silver Arrows continued their domination of Grand Prix racing. The Reims-Gueux circuit had its straights widened and facilities updated for the 1938 race. But when <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a> began, the French Grand Prix did not come back until 1947, where it was held at the one-time Parilly circuit near Lyon, a race that was marred by an accident involving <a href="/wiki/Pierre_Levegh" title="Pierre Levegh">Pierre Levegh</a> crashing into and killing 3 spectators. After that, Grand Prix racing returned to Reims-Gueux, where another manufacturer – Alfa Romeo – would dominate the event for 4 years. 1950 was the first year of the Formula One World Championship, but all the Formula One-regulated races were held in Europe. The race was won by Argentine <a href="/wiki/Juan_Manuel_Fangio" title="Juan Manuel Fangio">Juan Manuel Fangio</a>, who also won the next year's race – the longest Formula One race ever held in terms of distance covered, totalling 373 miles.
</p><p>The prestigious French event was held for the first time at the <a href="/wiki/Rouen-Les-Essarts" title="Rouen-Les-Essarts">Rouen-Les-Essarts</a> circuit in 1952, where it would be held four more times over the next 16 years. Rouen was a very high speed circuit located in the northern part of the country, that was made up mostly of high speed bends. But the race returned to Reims in 1953, where the triangular circuit, which was originally made up of three long straights (with a few slight kinks) two tight 90 degree right hand corners and a very slow right hand hairpin had been modified to bypass the town of Gueux, making it slightly faster. Reims now had two straights (including the even longer back straight), three very fast bends and two very slow and tight hairpins. This race was a classic, with Fangio in a Maserati and Briton <a href="/wiki/Mike_Hawthorn" title="Mike Hawthorn">Mike Hawthorn</a> in a Ferrari having a race-long battle for the lead, with Hawthorn taking the checkered flag. 1954 was another special event, and this marked Mercedes's return to top-flight road racing led by <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Neubauer" title="Alfred Neubauer">Alfred Neubauer</a>, 20 years after their first return to Grand Prix racing – in France. After two wins for the works Maserati team that year at Buenos Aires and Spa, Fangio was now driving for Mercedes and he and teammate Karl Kling effectively dominated the race from start to finish with their advanced <a href="/wiki/Mercedes-Benz_W196" title="Mercedes-Benz W196">W196</a>'s. It was not a popular win – Mercedes, a German car manufacturer, had won on French soil – only 9 years after the German occupation of France had ended. The French Grand Prix was cancelled in 1955 because of the <a href="/wiki/1955_Le_Mans_disaster" title="1955 Le Mans disaster">Le Mans disaster</a>, and Mercedes withdrew from all racing at the end of that year. The race continued to be held at Reims in 1956, another spell at a lengthened Rouen-Les-Essarts in 1957 and back to Reims again from 1958–1961, 1963 and one last event in 1966 at this circuit, located where champagne is made. The 1956 race saw a one-off appearance by Bugatti- they entered a new mid-engined Grand Prix car (which was a novelty at the time, and only the second Grand Prix car ever to be designed this way after the 1930s Auto Unions) designed by renowned Italian engineer Colombo and driven by <a href="/wiki/Maurice_Trintignant" title="Maurice Trintignant">Maurice Trintignant</a>, but the car was underpowered, overweight, and over-complicated, and it proved to be very difficult to drive; it retired early in the race. The 1958 race was marred by the fatal accident of Italian <a href="/wiki/Luigi_Musso" title="Luigi Musso">Luigi Musso</a>, driving a works Ferrari, and it was also Fangio's last Formula One race. Hawthorn, who like many other F1 drivers at the time, held Fangio in very high regard; and was about to lap Fangio (driving in an outdated Maserati) on the last lap on the pit straight when he slowed down and let Fangio cross the line before him so the respected Argentine driver could complete the whole race distance. Hawthorn won, and Fangio finished fourth.
</p><p>Rouen-Les-Essarts hosted the event in 1962 and 1964, and American <a href="/wiki/Dan_Gurney" title="Dan Gurney">Dan Gurney</a> won both these events, one in a Porsche and another in a Brabham. In 1965 the race was held at the 5.1 mile <a href="/wiki/Charade_Circuit" class="mw-redirect" title="Charade Circuit">Charade Circuit</a> in the hills surrounding Michelin's hometown of <a href="/wiki/Clermont-Ferrand" title="Clermont-Ferrand">Clermont-Ferrand</a> in central France. Unlike the long straights that made up Reims and the fast curves that made up Rouen, Charade was known as a mini-Nürburgring and was twisty, undulating and very demanding. The short <a href="/wiki/Circuit_de_la_Sarthe#Bugatti_Circuit" title="Circuit de la Sarthe">Bugatti Circuit</a> at Le Mans held the race in 1967, but the circuit was not liked by the Formula One circus, and it never returned. Rouen-Les-Essarts hosted the event in 1968, and it was a disastrous event; Frenchman <a href="/wiki/Jo_Schlesser" title="Jo Schlesser">Jo Schlesser</a> crashed and was killed at the very fast <i>Six Frères</i> corner in his burning Honda, and Formula One did not return to the public-road circuit. Charade hosted two more events, and then Formula One moved to the newly built, modern <a href="/wiki/Circuit_Paul_Ricard" title="Circuit Paul Ricard">Circuit Paul Ricard</a> on the French riviera for 1971. Paul Ricard Circuit, located in <a href="/wiki/Le_Castellet,_Var" title="Le Castellet, Var">Le Castellet</a>, just outside <a href="/wiki/Marseille" title="Marseille">Marseille</a> and not far from Monaco, was a new type of modern facility, much like Montlhéry had been in the 1920s. It had run-off areas, a wide track and ample viewing areas for spectators. Charade hosted the event one last time in <a href="/wiki/1972_French_Grand_Prix" title="1972 French Grand Prix">1972</a>; Formula One cars had become too fast for public road circuits; the circuit was littered with rocks and Austrian <a href="/wiki/Helmut_Marko" title="Helmut Marko">Helmut Marko</a> was hit in the eye by a rock thrown up from Brazilian <a href="/wiki/Emerson_Fittipaldi" title="Emerson Fittipaldi">Emerson Fittipaldi</a>'s Lotus which ended his racing career.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Paul_Ricard_and_Dijon-Prenois">Paul Ricard and Dijon-Prenois</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=French_Grand_Prix&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Paul Ricard and Dijon-Prenois">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Paul_Ricard_1986.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Paul_Ricard_1986.jpg/220px-Paul_Ricard_1986.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="189" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Paul_Ricard_1986.jpg/330px-Paul_Ricard_1986.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Paul_Ricard_1986.jpg/440px-Paul_Ricard_1986.jpg 2x" data-file-width="595" data-file-height="510" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Paul_Ricard_1986.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Paul Ricard short circuit, used from 1986 to 1990</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Dijon-Prenois_Circuit.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Dijon-Prenois_Circuit.svg/220px-Dijon-Prenois_Circuit.svg.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="220" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Dijon-Prenois_Circuit.svg/330px-Dijon-Prenois_Circuit.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Dijon-Prenois_Circuit.svg/440px-Dijon-Prenois_Circuit.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="2400" data-file-height="2400" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Dijon-Prenois_Circuit.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Dijon-Prenois, alternated with Paul Ricard from 1974 to 1984 (the short track was used in 1974)</div></div></div>
<p>Formula One returned to Paul Ricard in 1973; the French Grand Prix was never run on public road circuits like Reims, Rouen and Charade ever again. Paul Ricard also had a driving school, the École de Pilotage Winfield, run by the Knight brothers and Simon Delatour, that honed the talents of people such as France's first (and so far only) Formula One World Champion <a href="/wiki/Alain_Prost" title="Alain Prost">Alain Prost</a>, and Grand Prix winners <a href="/wiki/Didier_Pironi" title="Didier Pironi">Didier Pironi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jacques_Laffite" title="Jacques Laffite">Jacques Laffite</a>. The event was run at the new fast, up-and-down <a href="/wiki/Dijon-Prenois" title="Dijon-Prenois">Prenois</a> circuit near Dijon in 1974, before returning to Ricard in 1975 and 1976. The race was originally scheduled to be run at Clermont-Ferrand for 1974 and 1975, but the circuit was deemed too dangerous for Formula One. The two venues alternated the venue until 1984, with Ricard getting the race in even-numbered years and Dijon in odd-numbered years (except 1983). 1977 saw a new part of the Dijon circuit built called the "Parabolique". This was done to increase lap times which had been very nearly below a minute in 1974, and the race featured a battle between American Mario Andretti and Briton John Watson; Andretti came out on top to win. Lotus teammates Andretti and Swede Ronnie Peterson dominated the race in 1978 with their dominant 79s, a car that dominated the field in a way not seen since the dominating Alfa Romeo and domineering Ferrari in the early 1950s. The 1979 race was another classic, with the famous end-of-race duel for second place between Frenchman <a href="/wiki/Ren%C3%A9_Arnoux" title="René Arnoux">René Arnoux</a> in a 1.5-liter turbocharged <a href="/wiki/V6_engine" title="V6 engine">V6</a> Renault and Canadian <a href="/wiki/Gilles_Villeneuve" title="Gilles Villeneuve">Gilles Villeneuve</a> in a 3-liter <a href="/wiki/Boxer_engine" class="mw-redirect" title="Boxer engine">Flat-12</a> Ferrari. It is considered to be one of the all-time great duels in motorsports, with Arnoux and Villeneuve banging wheels and cars around the fast Dijon circuit before Villeneuve came out on top. The race was won by Arnoux's French teammate <a href="/wiki/Jean-Pierre_Jabouille" title="Jean-Pierre Jabouille">Jean-Pierre Jabouille</a>, which was the first race ever won by a Formula One car with a turbo-charged engine. 1980 saw rookie Prost qualify his slower McLaren seventh and Australian <a href="/wiki/Alan_Jones_(racing_driver)" title="Alan Jones (racing driver)">Alan Jones</a> beat French Ligier drivers Laffite and Pironi on their home soil, and the 1981 race was the first of 51 victories by future 4-time world champion Prost; driving a Renault, the famed French marque won the next three French Grands Prix. The 1982 event at Ricard was a memorable one for France – it was a turbo-charged engine/French walkover and 4 French drivers finished in the top 4 positions – each of them driving a car with a turbo-charged engine. Renault driver René Arnoux won from his teammate Prost and Ferrari drivers Pironi and <a href="/wiki/Patrick_Tambay" title="Patrick Tambay">Patrick Tambay</a> finished 3rd and 4th. But this French triumph was internally sour: Arnoux violated an agreement that if he was in front of Prost, he would let him by because Prost was better placed in the championship. Much to the chagrin of Prost and the French Renault team's management Arnoux did not do this, despite the management holding out pit boards ordering him to let Prost past. Prost won the next year at the same place, beating out <a href="/wiki/Nelson_Piquet" title="Nelson Piquet">Nelson Piquet</a> in a Brabham with a turbocharged BMW engine; Piquet had led the previous year's race but retired with engine failure.
</p><p>Dijon was last used in 1984, and by then turbo-charged engines were almost ubiquitous, save the Tyrrell team who were still using the Cosworth V8 engine. The international motorsports governing body at the time, FISA, had instituted a policy of long-term contracts with only one circuit per Grand Prix. The choice was between Dijon and Ricard – the small Prenois circuit had cars lapping in the 1 minute 1 second range, and Ricard was the main testing facility for Formula One at the time. So it was Ricard that was chosen, and it hosted the race from 1985–1990. From 1986 onwards Formula One used a shortened version of the circuit, after Elio de Angelis's fatal crash at the fast Verriere bends. De Angelis was not injured by the crash, however his car caught fire and there were no marshals to help him as it was a test session, and he died of smoke inhalation in hospital the next day. These two fast corners and the whole top section of the circuit was not used for the last five races. Prost won the final three races there, the 1988 one being a particularly dramatic win; he overtook his teammate <a href="/wiki/Ayrton_Senna" title="Ayrton Senna">Ayrton Senna</a> at the Curbe de Signes at the end of the ultra fast Mistral Straight and held onto the lead all the way to the finish, and the 1990 (by which time turbo-charged engines had been banned) event was led for more than 60 laps by Italian <a href="/wiki/Ivan_Capelli" title="Ivan Capelli">Ivan Capelli</a> and Brazilian <a href="/wiki/Maur%C3%ADcio_Gugelmin" title="Maurício Gugelmin">Maurício Gugelmin</a> in underfunded, <a href="/wiki/Adrian_Newey" title="Adrian Newey">Adrian Newey</a> designed Leyton-House cars – two cars that had failed to qualify at the <a href="/wiki/1990_Mexican_Grand_Prix" title="1990 Mexican Grand Prix">previous event</a> in Mexico. Prost, now driving for Ferrari after driving for McLaren from 1984–1989, made a late-race charge and passed Capelli to take the victory; Gugelmin had retired earlier.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Magny-Cours">Magny-Cours</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=French_Grand_Prix&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Magny-Cours">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Circuit_de_Nevers_Magny-Cours.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Circuit_de_Nevers_Magny-Cours.svg/220px-Circuit_de_Nevers_Magny-Cours.svg.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="152" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Circuit_de_Nevers_Magny-Cours.svg/330px-Circuit_de_Nevers_Magny-Cours.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Circuit_de_Nevers_Magny-Cours.svg/440px-Circuit_de_Nevers_Magny-Cours.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1356" data-file-height="939" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Circuit_de_Nevers_Magny-Cours.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Magny-Cours circuit, used from 1991 to 2008</div></div></div>
<p>In 1991, the race moved to the <a href="/wiki/Circuit_de_Nevers_Magny-Cours" title="Circuit de Nevers Magny-Cours">Circuit de Nevers Magny-Cours</a>, where it stayed for another 17 years. The move to Magny-Cours was an attempt to stimulate the economy of the area, but many within Formula One complained about the remote nature of the circuit. Highlights of Magny-Cours's time hosting the French Grand Prix include Prost's final of six wins on home soil in 1993, and Michael Schumacher's securing of the 2002 championship after only 11 races. The 2004 and 2005 races were in doubt because of financial problems and the addition of new circuits to the Formula One calendar. These races went ahead as planned, but it still had an uncertain future.
</p><p>In 2007 it was announced by the FFSA, the race promoter, that the 2008 French Grand Prix was put on an indefinite "pause". This suspension was due to the financial situation of the circuit, known to be disliked by many in F1 due to the circuit's location.<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> Then Bernie Ecclestone confirmed (at the time) that the <a href="/wiki/2007_French_Grand_Prix" title="2007 French Grand Prix">2007 French Grand Prix</a> would be the last to be held at Magny-Cours.<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> This turned out not to be true, because funding for the 2008 race was found, and this race at Magny-Cours was the last French Grand Prix for 10 years.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Absence">Absence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=French_Grand_Prix&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Absence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>After various negotiations, the future of the race at Magny-Cours took another turn, with increased speculation that the 2008 French Grand Prix would return, with Ecclestone himself stating "We're going to maybe resurrect it for a year, or something like that".<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> On 24 July, Ecclestone and the French Prime Minister met and agreed to possibly maintain the race at Magny Cours for 2008 and 2009.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> The change in fortune was completed on July, when the FIA published the <a href="/wiki/2008_Formula_One_World_Championship" title="2008 Formula One World Championship">2008</a> calendar with a 2008 French Grand Prix scheduled at Magny-Cours once again.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> The 2009 race, however, was again cancelled on 15 October 2008, with the official website citing "economic reasons".<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> A huge makeover of Magny-Cours ("2.0") was planned,<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> but cancelled in the end. The race's promoter FFSA then started looking for an alternative host. There were five different proposals for a new circuit: in <a href="/wiki/Rouen" title="Rouen">Rouen</a> with 3 possible layouts: a street circuit, in the dock area, or a permanent circuit near the airport,<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> a street circuit located near <a href="/wiki/Disneyland_Resort_Paris" class="mw-redirect" title="Disneyland Resort Paris">Disneyland Resort Paris</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Versailles,_Yvelines" title="Versailles, Yvelines">Versailles</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> and in <a href="/wiki/Sarcelles" title="Sarcelles">Sarcelles</a> (<i>Val de France</i>),<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> but all were cancelled. A final location in <a href="/wiki/Flins-Les_Mureaux" title="Flins-Les Mureaux">Flins-Les Mureaux</a>, near the <a href="/wiki/Flins_Renault_Factory" title="Flins Renault Factory">Flins Renault Factory</a> was being considered<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> however that was cancelled as well on 1 December 2009.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> In 2010 and 2011, there was no French Grand Prix on the Formula 1 calendar, although the <a href="/wiki/Circuit_Paul_Ricard" title="Circuit Paul Ricard">Circuit Paul Ricard</a> candidated itself for 2012.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>10 French drivers have won the French Grand Prix; 7 before World War I and II and 3 during the Formula One championship. French driver <a href="/wiki/Alain_Prost" title="Alain Prost">Alain Prost</a> won the race six times at three different circuits; however German driver <a href="/wiki/Michael_Schumacher" title="Michael Schumacher">Michael Schumacher</a> has won eight times – the most anybody has ever won any Grand Prix. Monégasque driver Louis Chiron won it five times, and the Argentine driver <a href="/wiki/Juan_Manuel_Fangio" title="Juan Manuel Fangio">Juan Manuel Fangio</a> and British driver <a href="/wiki/Nigel_Mansell" title="Nigel Mansell">Nigel Mansell</a> both won four times.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Return_to_Paul_Ricard">Return to Paul Ricard</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=French_Grand_Prix&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Return to Paul Ricard">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In December 2016, it was confirmed that the French Grand Prix would return in 2018 at the <a href="/wiki/Circuit_Paul_Ricard" title="Circuit Paul Ricard">Circuit Paul Ricard</a> and it currently holds a contract to host the French Grand Prix until at least 2022.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>
In an announcement to the nation on 13 April 2020, <a href="/wiki/Emmanuel_Macron" title="Emmanuel Macron">Emmanuel Macron</a>, the <a href="/wiki/French_president" class="mw-redirect" title="French president">French president</a>, said that restrictions on public events as a result of the <a href="/wiki/COVID-19_pandemic" title="COVID-19 pandemic">COVID-19 pandemic</a> would continue until mid-July, putting the <a href="/wiki/2020_French_Grand_Prix" class="mw-redirect" title="2020 French Grand Prix">2020 French Grand Prix</a>, scheduled for 28 June, at risk of postponement.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> The race was later cancelled with no intention to reschedule for the 2020 championship.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Official_names">Official names</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=French_Grand_Prix&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Official names">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<ul><li>1950, 1952–1954, 1956–1962, 1965: Grand Prix de l'A.C.F. <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Winners_of_the_French_Grand_Prix">Winners of the French Grand Prix</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=French_Grand_Prix&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Winners of the French Grand Prix">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span id="Repeat_winners_.28drivers.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Repeat_winners_(drivers)">Repeat winners (drivers)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=French_Grand_Prix&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Repeat winners (drivers)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><i>Drivers <b>in bold</b> are competing in the Formula One championship in the current season.</i><br />
<i>A pink background indicates an event which was not part of the Formula One World Championship.</i><br />
<i>A yellow background indicates an event which was part of the pre-<a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">war</a> European Championship.</i><br />
<i>A green background indicates an event which was part of the pre-war World Manufacturers' Championship</i>.
</p>

<span class="citation wikicite" id="endnote_1951"><b><a href="#ref_1951">^</a></b></span>  Juan Manuel Fangio won the 1951 race, but shared the win with <a href="/wiki/Luigi_Fagioli" title="Luigi Fagioli">Luigi Fagioli</a>.
</p>
<h3><span id="Repeat_winners_.28constructors.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Repeat_winners_(constructors)">Repeat winners (constructors)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=French_Grand_Prix&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Repeat winners (constructors)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><i>Teams <b>in bold</b> are competing in the Formula One championship in the current season.</i><br />
<i>A pink background indicates an event which was not part of the Formula One World Championship.</i><br />
<i>A yellow background indicates an event which was part of the pre-<a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">war</a> European Championship.</i><br />
<i>A green background indicates an event which was parto of the pre-war World Manufacturers' Championship</i>.
</p>

<h3><span id="Repeat_winners_.28engine_manufacturers.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Repeat_winners_(engine_manufacturers)">Repeat winners (engine manufacturers)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=French_Grand_Prix&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Repeat winners (engine manufacturers)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><i>Manufacturers <b>in bold</b> are competing in the Formula One championship in the current season.</i><br />
<i>A pink background indicates an event which was not part of the Formula One World Championship.</i><br />
<i>A yellow background indicates an event which was part of the pre-<a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">war</a> European Championship.</i><br />
<i>A green background indicates an event which was part of the pre-war World Manufacturers' Championship</i>.

<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: decimal;">
<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-83"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-83">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Two races which can be considered to be French Grand Prix were held in 1949. The race at Saint-Gaudens was organised by the ACF like all other French Grand Prix up to 1967, but was held for Sports Cars, whereas the race at Reims was organised as an alternative, and featured a much stronger grid.</span>
</li>
</ol></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Races_sometimes_considered_to_be_French_Grand_Prix">Races sometimes considered to be French Grand Prix</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=French_Grand_Prix&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Races sometimes considered to be French Grand Prix">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Beginning in the early 1920s, French media represented eight races held in France before 1906 as being Grands Prix de l'Automobile Club de France, leading to the first French Grand Prix being known as the ninth Grand Prix de l'ACF. This was done to give the Grand Prix the appearance of being the world's oldest motor race.<sup id="cite_ref-hodges_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hodges-84">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> The winners of these races, along with their original titles, are listed here.
</p>

</th>

</th>
<th>Constructor
</th>
<th>Location
</th>
<th>Report
</th></tr>
